` They shall not pass . ' ''
CHAPTER XX NORMAN DOUGLAS SPEAKS OUT IN MEETING `` Where be you wander , Anne o ' mine ? ''
ask the doctor , who even yet , after twenty-four year of marriage , occasionally address his wife thus when nobody be about .
Anne be sit on the veranda step , gaze absently over the wonderful bridal world of spring blossom , Beyond the white orchard be a copse of dark young fir and creamy wild cherry , where the robin be whistle madly ; for it be even and the fire of early star be burn over the maple grove .
Anne come back with a little sigh .
`` I be just take relief from intolerable reality in a dream , Gilbert -- a dream that all our child be home again -- and all small again -- play in Rainbow Valley .
It be always so silent now -- but I be imagine I hear clear voice and gay , childish sound come up a I use to .
I could hear Jem 's whistle and Walter 's yodel , and the twin ' laughter , and for just a few blessed minute I forget about the gun on the Western front , and have a little false , sweet happiness . ''
The doctor do not answer .
Sometimes his work trick him into forget for a few moment the Western front , but not often .
There be a good deal of grey now in his still thick curl that have not be there two year ago .
Yet he smile down into the starry eye he love -- the eye that have once be so full of laughter , and now seem always full of unshed tear .
Susan wander by with a hoe in her hand and her second best bonnet on her head .
`` I have just finish read a piece in the Enterprise which tell of a couple be marry in an aeroplane .
Do you think it would be legal , doctor dear ? ''
she inquire anxiously .
`` I think so , '' say the doctor gravely .
`` Well , '' say Susan dubiously , `` it seem to me that a wedding be too solemn for anything so giddy a an aeroplane .
But nothing be the same a it use to be .
Well , it be half an hour yet before prayer-meeting time , so I be go around to the kitchen garden to have a little evening hate with the weed .
But all the time I be strafe them I will be think about this new worry in the Trentino .
I do not like this Austrian caper , Mrs. Dr. dear . ''
`` Nor I , '' say Mrs. Blythe ruefully .
`` All the forenoon I preserve rhubarb with my hand and wait for the war news with my soul .
When it come I shrivel .
Well , I suppose I must go and get ready for the prayer-meeting , too . ''
Every village have it own little unwritten history , hand down from lip to lip through the generation , of tragic , comic , and dramatic event .
They be tell at wedding and festival , and rehearse around winter fireside .
And in these oral annals of Glen St. Mary the tale of the union prayer-meeting hold that night in the Methodist Church be destine to fill an imperishable place .
The union prayer-meeting be Mr. Arnold 's idea .
The county battalion , which have be train all winter in Charlottetown , be to leave shortly for overseas .
The Four Winds Harbour boys belong to it from the Glen and over-harbour and Harbour Head and Upper Glen be all home on their last leave , and Mr. Arnold think , properly enough , that it would be a fitting thing to hold a union prayer-meeting for them before they go away .
Mr. Meredith have agree , the meeting be announce to be hold in the Methodist Church .
Glen prayer-meetings be not apt to be too well attended , but on this particular even the Methodist Church be crowd .
Everybody who could go be there .
Even Miss Cornelia come -- and it be the first time in her life that Miss Cornelia have ever set foot inside a Methodist Church .
It take no less than a world conflict to bring that about .
`` I use to hate Methodists , '' say Miss Cornelia calmly , when her husband express surprise over her go , `` but I do n't hate them now .
There be no sense in hat Methodists when there be a Kaiser or a Hindenburg in the world . ''
So Miss Cornelia go .
Norman Douglas and his wife go too .
And Whiskers-on-the-moon strut up the aisle to a front pew , a if he fully realize what a distinction he confer upon the building .
People be somewhat surprised that he should be there , since he usually avoid all assemblage connect in any way with the war .
But Mr. Meredith have say that he hop his session would be well represent , and Mr. Pryor have evidently take the request to heart .
He wear his best black suit and white tie , his thick , tight , iron-grey curl be neatly arrange , and his broad , red round face look , a Susan most uncharitably think , more `` sanctimonious '' than ever .
`` The minute I saw that man come into the Church , look like that , I felt that mischief be brewing , Mrs. Dr. dear , '' she say afterwards .
`` What form it would take I could not tell , but I know from face of him that he have come there for no good . ''
The prayer-meeting open conventionally and continue quietly .
Mr. Meredith speak first with his usual eloquence and feeling .
Mr. Arnold follow with an address which even Miss Cornelia have to confess be irreproachable in taste and subject-matter .
And then Mr. Arnold ask Mr. Pryor to lead in prayer .
Miss Cornelia have always aver that Mr. Arnold have no gumption .
Miss Cornelia be not apt to err on the side of charity in her judgment of Methodist minister , but in this case she do not greatly overshoot the mark .
The Rev . Mr. Arnold certainly do not have much of that desirable , indefinable quality know a gumption , or he would never have ask Whiskers-on-the-moon to lead in prayer at a khaki prayer-meeting .
He think he be return the compliment to Mr. Meredith , who , at the conclusion of his address , have ask a Methodist deacon to lead .
Some people expect Mr. Pryor to refuse grumpily -- and that would have make enough scandal .
But Mr. Pryor bound briskly to his foot , unctuously say , `` Let u pray , '' and forthwith prayed .
In a sonorous voice which penetrate to every corner of the crowded building Mr. Pryor pour forth a flood of fluent word , and be well on in his prayer before his dazed and horrify audience awaken to the fact that they be listen to a pacifist appeal of the rank sort .
Mr. Pryor have at least the courage of his conviction ; or perhaps , a people afterwards say , he think he be safe in a church and that it be an excellent chance to air certain opinion he dare not voice elsewhere , for fear of be mob .
He pray that the unholy war might cease -- that the deluded army be drive to slaughter on the Western front might have their eye open to their iniquity and repent while yet there be time -- that the poor young men present in khaki , who have be hound into a path of murder and militarism , should yet be rescue -- Mr. Pryor have get this far without let or hindrance ; and so paralysed be his hearer , and so deeply imbue with their born-and-bred conviction that no disturbance must ever be make in a church , no matter what the provocation , that it seem likely that he would continue unchecked to the end .
But one man at least in that audience be not hamper by inherited or acquire reverence for the sacred edifice .
Norman Douglas be , a Susan have often vow crisply , nothing more or less than a `` pagan . ''
But he be a rampantly patriotic pagan , and when the significance of what Mr. Pryor be say fully dawn on him , Norman Douglas suddenly go berserk .
With a positive roar he bound to his foot in his side pew , face the audience , and shout in tone of thunder : `` Stop -- stop -- STOP that abominable prayer !
What an abominable prayer ! ''
Every head in the church fly up .
A boy in khaki at the back give a faint cheer .
Mr. Meredith raise a deprecating hand , but Norman be past caring for anything like that .
Eluding his wife 's restrain grasp , he give one mad spring over the front of the pew and catch the unfortunate Whiskers-on-the-moon by his coat collar .
Mr. Pryor have not `` stop '' when so bidden , but he stop now , perforce , for Norman , his long red beard literally bristle with fury , be shake him until his bone fairly rattle , and punctuate his shake with a lurid assortment of abusive epithet .
`` You blatant beast ! ''
-- shake -- `` You malignant carrion '' -- shake -- `` You pig-headed varmint ! ''
-- shake -- `` you putrid pup '' -- shake -- `` you pestilential parasite '' -- shake -- `` you -- Hunnish scum '' -- shake -- `` you indecent reptile -- you -- you -- '' Norman choke for a moment .
Everybody believe that the next thing he would say , church or no church , would be something that would have to be spell with asterisk ; but at that moment Norman encounter his wife 's eye and he fell back with a thud on Holy Writ .
`` You white sepulchre ! ''
he bellow , with a final shake , and cast Whiskers-on-the-moon from him with a vigour which impel that unhappy pacifist to the very verge of the choir entrance door .
Mr. Pryor 's once ruddy face be ashen .
But he turn at bay .
`` I 'll have the law on you for this , '' he gasp .
`` Do -- do , '' roar Norman , make another rush .
But Mr. Pryor be go .
He have no desire to fall a second time into the hand of an avenging militarist .
Norman turn to the platform for one graceless , triumphant moment .
`` Do n't look so flabbergasted , parson , '' he boom .
`` You could n't do it -- nobody would expect it of the cloth -- but somebody have to do it .
You know you 're glad I throw him out -- he could n't be let go on yammering and yodelling and yawp sedition and treason .
Sedition and treason -- somebody have to deal with it .
I be bear for this hour -- I 've have my inning in church at last .
I can sit quiet for another sixty year now !
Go ahead with your meeting , parson .
I reckon you wo n't be trouble with any more pacifist prayer . ''
But the spirit of devotion and reverence have flee .
Both minister realize it and realize that the only thing to do be to close the meeting quietly and let the excited people go .
Mr. Meredith address a few earnest word to the boy in khaki -- which probably save Mr. Pryor 's window from a second onslaught -- and Mr. Arnold pronounce an incongruous benediction , at least he felt it be incongruous , for he could not at once banish from his memory the sight of gigantic Norman Douglas shake the fat , pompous little Whiskers-on-the-moon a a huge mastiff might shake an overgrown puppy .
And he know that the same picture be in everybody 's mind .
Altogether the union prayer-meeting could hardly be call an unqualified success .
But it be remember in Glen St. Mary when score of orthodox and undisturbed assembly be totally forget .
`` You will never , no , never , Mrs. Dr. dear , hear me call Norman Douglas a pagan again , '' say Susan when she reach home .
`` If Ellen Douglas be not a proud woman this night she should be . ''
`` Norman Douglas do a wholly indefensible thing , '' say the doctor .
`` Pryor should have be let severely alone until the meeting be over .
Then later on , his own minister and session should deal with him .
That would have be the proper procedure .
Norman 's performance be utterly improper and scandalous and outrageous ; but , by George , '' -- the doctor throw back his head and chuckle , `` by George , Anne-girl , it be satisfy . ''
CHAPTER XXI `` LOVE AFFAIRS ARE HORRIBLE '' Ingleside 20th June 1916 `` We have be so busy , and day after day have bring such exciting news , good and bad , that I have n't have time and composure to write in my diary for week .
I like to keep it up regularly , for father say a diary of the year of the war should be a very interesting thing to hand down to one 's child .
The trouble be , I like to write a few personal thing in this bless old book that might not be exactly what I 'd want my child to read .
I feel that I shall be a far great stickler for propriety in regard to them than I be for myself !
`` The first week in June be another dreadful one .
The Austrians seem just on the point of overrun Italy : and then come the first awful news of the Battle of Jutland , which the Germans claim a a great victory .
Susan be the only one who carry on .
` You need never tell me that the Kaiser have defeat the British Navy , ' she say , with a contemptuous sniff .
` It be all a German lie and that you may tie to . '
And when a couple of day later we find out that she be right and that it have be a British victory instead of a British defeat , we have to put up with a great many ' I tell you so 's , ' but we endure them very comfortably .
`` It take Kitchener 's death to finish Susan .
For the first time I saw her down and out .
We all felt the shock of it but Susan plumb the depth of despair .
The news come at night by ` phone but Susan would n't believe it until she saw the Enterprise headline the next day .
She do not cry or faint or go into hysteric ; but she forget to put salt in the soup , and that be something Susan never do in my recollection .
Mother and Miss Oliver and I cry but Susan look at u in stony sarcasm and say , ` The Kaiser and his six son be all alive and thriving .
So the world be not leave wholly desolate .
Why cry , Mrs. Dr. dear ? '
Susan continue in this stony , hopeless condition for twenty-four hour , and then Cousin Sophia appear and begin to condole with her . ''
` This be terrible news , ai n't it , Susan ?
We might as well prepare for the bad for it be bind to come .
You say once -- and well do I remember the word , Susan Baker -- that you have complete confidence in God and Kitchener .
Ah well , Susan Baker , there be only God leave now . '
`` Whereat Cousin Sophia put her handkerchief to her eye pathetically a if the world be indeed in terrible strait .
As for Susan , Cousin Sophia be the salvation of her .
She come to life with a jerk . ''
` Sophia Crawford , hold your peace ! '
she say sternly .
` You may be an idiot but you need not be an irreverent idiot .
It be no more than decent to be weep and wail because the Almighty be the sole stay of the Allies now .
As for Kitchener , his death be a great loss and I do not dispute it .
But the outcome of this war do not depend on one man 's life and now that the Russians be come on again you will soon see a change for the good . '
`` Susan say this so energetically that she convince herself and cheer up immediately .
But Cousin Sophia shake her head . ''
` Albert 's wife want to call the baby after Brusiloff , ' she say , ` but I tell her to wait and see what becomes of him first .
Them Russians have such a habit of petering out . '
`` The Russians be do splendidly , however , and they have save Italy .
But even when the daily news of their sweeping advance come we do n't feel like run up the flag a we use to do .
As Gertrude say , Verdun have slay all exultation .
We would all feel more like rejoice if the victory be on the western front .
` When will the British strike ? '
Gertrude sigh this morning .
` We have wait so long -- so long . '
`` Our great local event in recent week be the route march the county battalion make through the county before it leave for overseas .
They march from Charlottetown to Lowbridge , then round the Harbour Head and through the Upper Glen and so down to the St. Mary station .
Everybody turn out to see them , except old Aunt Fannie Clow , who be bedridden and Mr. Pryor , who have n't be see out even in church since the night of the Union Prayer Meeting the previous week .
`` It be wonderful and heartbreaking to see that battalion march past .
There be young men and middle-aged men in it .
There be Laurie McAllister from over-harbour who be only sixteen but swear he be eighteen , so that he could enlist ; and there be Angus Mackenzie , from the Upper Glen who be fifty-five if he be a day and swore he be forty-four .
There be two South African veteran from Lowbridge , and the three eighteen-year-old Baxter triplet from Harbour Head .
Everybody cheer a they go by , and they cheer Foster Booth , who be forty , walk side by side with his son Charley who be twenty .
Charley 's mother die when he be bear , and when Charley enlist Foster say he 'd never yet let Charley go anywhere he dare n't go himself , and he do n't mean to begin with the Flanders trench .
At the station Dog Monday nearly go out of his head .
He tear about and send message to Jem by them all .
Mr. Meredith read an address and Reta Crawford recite ` The Piper . '
The soldier cheer her like mad and cried ` We 'll follow -- we 'll follow -- we wo n't break faith , ' and I felt so proud to think that it be my dear brother who have write such a wonderful , heart-stirring thing .
And then I look at the khaki rank and wonder if those tall fellow in uniform could be the boy I 've laugh with and play with and dance with and tease all my life .
Something seem to have touch them and set them apart .
They have hear the Piper 's call .
`` Fred Arnold be in the battalion and I felt dreadfully about him , for I realize that it be because of me that he be go away with such a sorrowful expression .
I could n't help it but I felt as badly a if I could .
`` The last evening of his leave Fred come up to Ingleside and tell me he love me and ask me if I would promise to marry him some day , if he ever come back .
He be desperately in earnest and I felt more wretched than I ever do in my life .
I could n't promise him that -- why , even if there be no question of Ken , I do n't care for Fred that way and never could -- but it seem so cruel and heartless to send him away to the front without any hope of comfort .
I cry like a baby ; and yet -- oh , I be afraid that there must be something incurably frivolous about me , because , right in the middle of it all , with me cry and Fred look so wild and tragic , the thought pop into my head that it would be an unendurable thing to see that nose across from me at the breakfast table every morning of my life .
There , that be one of the entry I would n't want my descendant to read in this journal .
But it be the humiliate truth ; and perhaps it 's just as well that think do come or I might have be trick by pity and remorse into give him some rash assurance .
If Fred 's nose be as handsome a his eye and mouth some such thing might have happen .
And then what an unthinkable predicament I should have be in !
`` When poor Fred become convinced that I could n't promise him , he behave beautifully -- though that rather make thing worse .
If he have be nasty about it I would n't have felt so heartbroken and remorseful -- though why I should feel remorseful I do n't know , for I never encourage Fred to think I care a bit about him .
Yet feel remorseful I do -- and do .
If Fred Arnold never come back from overseas , this will haunt me all my life .
`` Then Fred say if he could n't take my love with him to the trench at least he want to feel that he have my friendship , and would I kiss him just once in good-bye before he go -- perhaps for ever ?
`` I do n't know how I could ever have imagine that love affair be delightful , interesting thing .
They be horrible .
I could n't even give poor heartbroken Fred one little kiss , because of my promise to Ken .
It seem so brutal .
I have to tell Fred that of course he would have my friendship , but that I could n't kiss him because I have promise somebody else I would n't .
`` He say , ` It be -- be it -- Ken Ford ? '
`` I nod .
It seem dreadful to have to tell it -- it be such a sacred little secret just between me and Ken .
`` When Fred go away I come up here to my room and cry so long and so bitterly that mother come up and insist on know what be the matter .
I tell her .
She listen to my tale with an expression that clearly say , ` Can it be possible that anyone have be want to marry this baby ? '
But she be so nice and understanding and sympathetic , oh , just so race-of-Josephy -- that I felt indescribably comfort .
Mothers be the dear thing . ''
` But oh , mother , ' I sob , ` he want me to kiss him good-bye -- and I could n't -- and that hurt me bad than all the rest . ' ''
` Well , why do n't you kiss him ? '
ask mother coolly .
` Considering the circumstance , I think you might have . ' ''
` But I could n't , mother -- I promise Ken when he go away that I would n't kiss anybody else until he come back . '
`` This be another high explosive for poor mother .
She exclaim , with the queer little catch in her voice , ` Rilla , be you engage to Kenneth Ford ? ' ''
` I -- do n't -- know , ' I sob . ''
` You -- do n't -- know ? '
repeat mother .
`` Then I have to tell her the whole story , too ; and every time I tell it it seem silly and silly to imagine that Ken mean anything serious .
I felt idiotic and ashamed by the time I get through .
`` Mother sit a little while in silence .
Then she come over , sit down beside me , and take me in her arm . ''
` Do n't cry , dear little Rilla-my-Rilla .
You have nothing to reproach yourself with in regard to Fred ; and if Leslie West 's son ask you to keep your lip for him , I think you may consider yourself engage to him .
But -- oh , my baby -- my last little baby -- I have lose you -- the war have make a woman of you too soon . '
`` I shall never be too much of a woman to find comfort in mother 's hug .
Nevertheless , when I saw Fred marching by two day later in the parade , my heart ache unbearably .
`` But I 'm glad mother think I 'm really engage to Ken ! ''
CHAPTER XXII LITTLE DOG MONDAY KNOWS `` It be two year tonight since the dance at the light , when Jack Elliott bring u news of the war .
Do you remember , Miss Oliver ? ''
Cousin Sophia answer for Miss Oliver .
`` Oh , indeed , Rilla , I remember that even only too well , and you a-prancing down here to show off your party clothes .
Did n't I warn you that we could not tell what be before u ?
Little do you think that night what be before you . ''
`` Little do any of u think that , '' say Susan sharply , `` not be gift with the power of prophecy .
It do not require any great foresight , Sophia Crawford , to tell a body that she will have some trouble before her life be over .
I could do as much myself . ''
`` We all think the war would be over in a few month then , '' say Rilla wistfully .
`` When I look back it seem so ridiculous that we ever could have suppose it . ''
`` And now , two year later , it be no nearer the end than it be then , '' say Miss Oliver gloomily .
Susan click her knitting-needles briskly .
`` Now , Miss Oliver , dear , you know that be not a reasonable remark .
You know we be just two year nearer the end , whenever the end be appoint to be . ''
`` Albert read in a Montreal paper today that a war expert give it a his opinion that it will last five year more , '' be Cousin Sophia 's cheerful contribution .
`` It ca n't , '' cry Rilla ; then she add with a sigh , `` Two year ago we would have say ` It ca n't last two year . '
But five more year of this ! ''
`` If Rumania come in , a I have strong hope now of her do , you will see the end in five month instead of five year , '' say Susan .
`` I 've no faith in furriners , '' sigh Cousin Sophia .
`` The French be foreigner , '' retort Susan , `` and look at Verdun .
And think of all the Somme victory this bless summer .
The Big Push be on and the Russians be still go well .
Why , General Haig say that the German officer he have capture admit that they have lose the war . ''
`` You ca n't believe a word the Germans say , '' protest Cousin Sophia .
`` There be no sense in believe a thing just because you 'd like to believe it , Susan Baker .
The British have lose million of men at the Somme and how far have they get ?
Look facts in the face , Susan Baker , look fact in the face . ''
`` They be wear the Germans out and so long a that happen it do not matter whether it be do a few mile east or a few mile west .
I be not , '' admit Susan in tremendous humility , `` I be not a military expert , Sophia Crawford , but even I can see that , and so could you if you be not determine to take a gloomy view of everything .
The Huns have not get all the cleverness in the world .
Have you not hear the story of Alistair MacCallum 's son Roderick , from the Upper Glen ?
He be a prisoner in Germany and his mother get a letter from him last week .
He write that he be be very kindly treat and that all the prisoner have plenty of food and so on , till you would have suppose everything be lovely .
But when he sign his name , right in between Roderick and MacCallum , he write two Gaelic word that mean ` all lie ' and the German censor do not understand Gaelic and think it be all part of Roddy 's name .
So he let it pass , never dream how he be diddle .
Well , I be go to leave the war to Haig for the rest of the day and make a frosting for my chocolate cake .
And when it be make I shall put it on the top shelf .
The last one I make I leave it on the low shelf and little Kitchener sneak in and claw all the ice off and eat it .
We have company for tea that night and when I go to get my cake what a sight do I behold ! ''
`` Has that pore orphan 's father never be heerd from yet ? ''
ask Cousin Sophia .
`` Yes , I have a letter from him in July , '' say Rilla .
`` He say that when he get word of his wife 's death and of my take the baby -- Mr. Meredith write him , you know -- he write right away , but a he never get any answer he have begin to think his letter must have be lose . ''
`` It take him two year to begin to think it , '' say Susan scornfully .
`` Some people think very slow .
Jim Anderson have not get a scratch , for all he have be two year in the trench .
A fool for luck , a the old proverb say . ''
`` He write very nicely about Jims and say he 'd like to see him , '' say Rilla .
`` So I write and tell him all about the wee man , and send him snapshot .
Jims will be two year old next week and he be a perfect duck . ''
`` You do n't use to be very fond of baby , '' say Cousin Sophia .
`` I 'm not a bit fonder of baby in the abstract than ever I be , '' say Rilla , frankly .
`` But I do love Jims , and I 'm afraid I be n't really half as glad a I should have be when Jim Anderson 's letter prove that he be safe and sound . ''
`` You be n't hop the man would be kill ! ''
cry Cousin Sophia in horrified accent .
`` No -- no -- no !
I just hop he would go on forget about Jims , Mrs. Crawford . ''
`` And then your pa would have the expense of raise him , '' say Cousin Sophia reprovingly .
`` You young creeturs be terrible thoughtless . ''
Jims himself run in at this juncture , so rosy and curly and kissable , that he extort a qualified compliment even from Cousin Sophia .
`` He 's a reel healthy-looking child now , though mebbee his colour be a mite too high -- sorter consumptive look , a you might say .
I never think you 'd raise him when I saw him the day after you brung him home .
I reely do not think it be in you and I tell Albert 's wife so when I get home .
Albert 's wife say , say she , ` There 's more in Rilla Blythe than you 'd think for , Aunt Sophia . '
Them be her very word .
` More in Rilla Blythe than you 'd think for . '
Albert 's wife always have a good opinion of you . ''
Cousin Sophia sigh , a if to imply that Albert 's wife stand alone in this against the world .
But Cousin Sophia really do not mean that .
She be quite fond of Rilla in her own melancholy way ; but young creeturs have to be keep down .
If they be not keep down society would be demoralize .
`` Do you remember your walk home from the light two year ago tonight ? ''
whisper Gertrude Oliver to Rilla , teasingly .
`` I should think I do , '' smile Rilla ; and then her smile grow dreamy and absent ; she be remember something else -- that hour with Kenneth on the sandshore .
Where would Ken be tonight ?
And Jem and Jerry and Walter and all the other boy who have dance and moonlight on the old Four Winds Point that evening of mirth and laughter -- their last joyous unclouded evening .
In the filthy trench of the Somme front , with the roar of the gun and the groan of stricken men for the music of Ned Burr 's violin , and the flash of star shell for the silver sparkle on the old blue gulf .
Two of them be sleep under the Flanders poppy -- Alec Burr from the Upper Glen , and Clark Manley of Lowbridge .
Others be wound in the hospital .
But so far nothing have touch the manse and the Ingleside boy .
They seem to bear charmed life .
Yet the suspense never grow any easy to bear a the week and month of war go by .
`` It be n't a if it be some sort of fever to which you might conclude they be immune when they have n't take it for two year , '' sigh Rilla .
`` The danger be just as great and just as real a it be the first day they go into the trench .
I know this , and it torture me every day .
And yet I ca n't help hop that since they 've come this far unhurt they 'll come through .
Oh , Miss Oliver , what would it be like not to wake up in the morning feeling afraid of the news the day would bring ?
I ca n't picture such a state of thing somehow .
And two year ago this morning I wake wonder what delightful gift the new day would give me .
These be the two year I think would be fill with fun . ''
`` Would you exchange them -- now -- for two year fill with fun ? ''
`` No , '' say Rilla slowly .
`` I would n't .
It 's strange -- be n't it ?
-- They have be two terrible year -- and yet I have a queer feeling of thankfulness for them -- a if they have bring me something very precious , with all their pain .
I would n't want to go back and be the girl I be two year ago , not even if I could .
Not that I think I 've make any wonderful progress -- but I 'm not quite the selfish , frivolous little doll I be then .
I suppose I have a soul then , Miss Oliver -- but I do n't know it .
I know it now -- and that be worth a great deal -- worth all the suffering of the past two year .
And still '' -- Rilla give a little apologetic laugh , `` I do n't want to suffer any more -- not even for the sake of more soul growth .
At the end of two more year I might look back and be thankful for the development they have bring me , too ; but I do n't want it now . ''
`` We never do , '' say Miss Oliver .
`` That be why we be not leave to choose our own mean and measure of development , I suppose .
No matter how much we value what our lesson have bring u we do n't want to go on with the bitter schooling .
Well , let u hope for the best , a Susan say ; thing be really go well now and if Rumania line up , the end may come with a suddenness that will surprise u all . ''
Rumania do come in -- and Susan remark approvingly that it king and queen be the fine look royal couple she have see picture of .
So the summer pass away .
Early in September word come that the Canadians have be shift to the Somme front and anxiety grow tenser and deeper .
For the first time Mrs. Blythe 's spirit fail her a little , and a the day of suspense wore on the doctor begin to look gravely at her , and veto this or that special effort in Red Cross work .
`` Oh , let me work -- let me work , Gilbert , '' she entreat feverishly .
`` While I 'm work I do n't think so much .
If I 'm idle I imagine everything -- rest be only torture for me .
My two boy be on the frightful Somme front -- and Shirley pore day and night over aviation literature and say nothing .
But I see the purpose grow in his eye .
No , I can not rest -- do n't ask it of me , Gilbert . ''
But the doctor be inexorable .
`` I ca n't let you kill yourself , Anne-girl , '' he say .
`` When the boy come back I want a mother here to welcome them .
Why , you 're get transparent .
It wo n't do -- ask Susan there if it will do . ''
`` Oh , if Susan and you be both band together against me ! ''
say Anne helplessly .
One day the glorious news come that the Canadians have take Courcelette and Martenpuich , with many prisoner and gun .
Susan run up the flag and say it be plain to be see that Haig know what soldier to pick for a hard job .
The others dare not feel exultant .
Who know what price have be pay ?
Rilla wake that morning when the dawn be begin to break and go to her window to look out , her thick creamy eyelid heavy with sleep .
Just at dawn the world look a it never look at any other time .
The air be cold with dew and the orchard and grove and Rainbow Valley be full of mystery and wonder .
Over the eastern hill be golden deep and silvery-pink shallow .
There be no wind , and Rilla hear distinctly a dog howling in a melancholy way down in the direction of the station .
Was it Dog Monday ?
And if it be , why be he howl like that ?
Rilla shiver ; the sound have something boding and grievous in it .
She remember that Miss Oliver say once , when they be come home in the darkness and hear a dog howl , `` When a dog cry like that the Angel of Death be pass . ''
Rilla listen with a curdle fear at her heart .
It be Dog Monday -- she felt sure of it .
Whose dirge be he howl -- to whose spirit be he send that anguish greeting and farewell ?
Rilla go back to bed but she could not sleep .
All day she watch and wait in a dread of which she do not speak to anyone .
She go down to see Dog Monday and the station-master say , `` That dog of yours howl from midnight to sunrise something weird .
I dunno what get into him .
I get up once and go out and holler at him but he pay no ` tention to me .
He be sit all alone in the moonlight out there at the end of the platform , and every few minute the poor lonely little beggar 'd lift his nose and howl a if his heart be break .
He never do it afore -- always sleep in his kennel real quiet and canny from train to train .
But he sure have something on his mind last night . ''
Dog Monday be lie in his kennel .
He wag his tail and lick Rilla 's hand .
But he would not touch the food she bring for him .
`` I 'm afraid he 's sick , '' she say anxiously .
She hat to go away and leave him .
But no bad news come that day -- nor the next -- nor the next .
Rilla 's fear lift .
Dog Monday howl no more and resume his routine of train meeting and watching .
When five day have pass the Ingleside people begin to feel that they might be cheerful again .
Rilla dash about the kitchen help Susan with the breakfast and sing so sweetly and clearly that Cousin Sophia across the road hear her and croak out to Mrs. Albert , '' ` Sing before eating , cry before sleep , ' I 've always hear . ''
But Rilla Blythe shed no tear before the nightfall .
When her father , his face grey and drawn and old , come to her that afternoon and tell her that Walter have be kill in action at Courcelette she crumple up in a pitiful little heap of merciful unconsciousness in his arm .
Nor do she waken to her pain for many hour .
CHAPTER XXIII `` AND SO , GOODNIGHT '' The fierce flame of agony have burn itself out and the grey dust of it ash be over all the world .
Rilla 's young life recover physically sooner than her mother .
For week Mrs. Blythe lay ill from grief and shock .
Rilla find it be possible to go on with existence , since existence have still to be reckon with .
There be work to be do , for Susan could not do all .
For her mother 's sake she have to put on calmness and endurance a a garment in the day ; but night after night she lay in her bed , weep the bitter rebellious tear of youth until at last tear be all weep out and the little patient ache that be to be in her heart until she die take their place .
She cling to Miss Oliver , who know what to say and what not to say .
So few people do .
Kind , well-meaning caller and comforter give Rilla some terrible moment .
`` You 'll get over it in time , '' Mrs. William Reese say , cheerfully .
Mrs. Reese have three stalwart son , not one of whom have go to the front .
`` It 's such a bless it be Walter who be take and not Jem , '' say Miss Sarah Clow .
`` Walter be a member of the church , and Jem be n't .
I 've tell Mr. Meredith many a time that he should have speak seriously to Jem about it before he go away . ''
`` Pore , pore Walter , '' sigh Mrs. Reese .
`` Do not you come here call him poor Walter , '' say Susan indignantly , appear in the kitchen door , much to the relief of Rilla , who felt that she could endure no more just then .
`` He be not poor .
He be rich than any of you .
It be you who stay at home and will not let your son go who be poor -- poor and naked and mean and small -- pisen poor , and so be your son , with all their prosperous farm and fat cattle and their souls no big than a flea 's -- if a big . ''
`` I come here to comfort the afflicted and not to be insult , '' say Mrs. Reese , take her departure , unregretted by anyone .
Then the fire go out of Susan and she retreat to her kitchen , lay her faithful old head on the table and weep bitterly for a time .
Then she go to work and iron Jims 's little romper .
Rilla scold her gently for it when she herself come in to do it .
`` I be not go to have you kill yourself work for any war-baby , '' Susan say obstinately .
`` Oh , I wish I could just keep on work all the time , Susan , '' cry poor Rilla .
`` And I wish I do n't have to go to sleep .
It be hideous to go to sleep and forget it for a little while , and wake up and have it all rush over me anew the next morning .
Do people ever get use to thing like this , Susan ?
And oh , Susan , I ca n't get away from what Mrs. Reese say .
Did Walter suffer much -- he be always so sensitive to pain .
Oh , Susan , if I know that he do n't I think I could gather up a little courage and strength . ''
This merciful knowledge be give to Rilla .
A letter come from Walter 's commanding officer , tell them that he have be kill instantly by a bullet during a charge at Courcelette .
The same day there be a letter for Rilla from Walter himself .
Rilla carry it unopened to Rainbow Valley and read it there , in the spot where she have have her last talk with him .
It be a strange thing to read a letter after the writer be dead -- a bitter-sweet thing , in which pain and comfort be strangely mingle .
For the first time since the blow have fall Rilla felt -- a different thing from tremulous hope and faith -- that Walter , of the glorious gift and the splendid ideal , still live , with just the same gift and just the same ideal .
That could not be destroy -- these could suffer no eclipse .
The personality that have express itself in that last letter , write on the eve of Courcelette , could not be snuff out by a German bullet .
It must carry on , though the earthly link with thing of earth be break .
`` We 're go over the top tomorrow , Rilla-my-Rilla , '' write Walter .
`` I write mother and Di yesterday , but somehow I feel a if I must write you tonight .
I have n't intend to do any writing tonight -- but I 've get to .
Do you remember old Mrs. Tom Crawford over-harbour , who be always say that it be ` laid on her ' to do such and such a thing ?
Well , that be just how I feel .
It 's ` lay on me ' to write you tonight -- you , sister and chum of mine .
There be some thing I want to say before -- well , before tomorrow .
`` You and Ingleside seem strangely near me tonight .
It 's the first time I 've felt this since I come .
Always home have seem so far away -- so hopelessly far away from this hideous welter of filth and blood .
But tonight it be quite close to me -- it seem to me I can almost see you -- hear you speak .
And I can see the moonlight shin white and still on the old hill of home .
It have seem to me ever since I come here that it be impossible that there could be calm gentle night and unshattered moonlight anywhere in the world .
But tonight somehow , all the beautiful thing I have always love seem to have become possible again -- and this be good , and make me feel a deep , certain , exquisite happiness .
It must be autumn at home now -- the harbour be a-dream and the old Glen hill blue with haze , and Rainbow Valley a haunt of delight with wild aster blow all over it -- our old `` farewell-summers . ''
I always like that name well than ` aster ' -- it be a poem in itself .
`` Rilla , you know I 've always have premonition .
You remember the Pied Piper -- but no , of course you would n't -- you be too young .
One evening long ago when Nan and Di and Jem and the Merediths and I be together in Rainbow Valley I have a queer vision or presentiment -- whatever you like to call it .
Rilla , I saw the Piper come down the Valley with a shadowy host behind him .
The others think I be only pretend -- but I saw him for just one moment .
And Rilla , last night I saw him again .
I be do sentry-go and I saw him march across No-man 's - land from our trench to the German trench -- the same tall shadowy form , pip weirdly -- and behind him follow boy in khaki .
Rilla , I tell you I saw him -- it be no fancy -- no illusion .
I hear his music , and then -- he be go .
But I have see him -- and I know what it mean -- I know that I be among those who follow him .
`` Rilla , the Piper will pipe me ` west ' tomorrow .
I feel sure of this .
And Rilla , I 'm not afraid .
When you hear the news , remember that .
I 've win my own freedom here -- freedom from all fear .
I shall never be afraid of anything again -- not of death -- nor of life , if after all , I be to go on living .
And life , I think , would be the harder of the two to face -- for it could never be beautiful for me again .
There would always be such horrible thing to remember -- thing that would make life ugly and painful always for me .
I could never forget them .
But whether it 's life or death , I 'm not afraid , Rilla-my-Rilla , and I be not sorry that I come .
I 'm satisfied .
I 'll never write the poem I once dream of writing -- but I 've help to make Canada safe for the poet of the future -- for the worker of the future -- ay , and the dreamer , too -- for if no man dream , there will be nothing for the worker to fulfil -- the future , not of Canada only but of the world -- when the ` red rain ' of Langemarck and Verdun shall have bring forth a golden harvest -- not in a year or two , a some foolishly think , but a generation later , when the seed sown now shall have have time to germinate and grow .
Yes , I 'm glad I come , Rilla .
It be n't only the fate of the little sea-born island I love that be in the balance -- nor of Canada nor of England .
It 's the fate of mankind .
That be what we 're fight for .
And we shall win -- never for a moment doubt that , Rilla .
For it be n't only the living who be fight -- the dead be fight too .
Such an army can not be defeat .
`` Is there laughter in your face yet , Rilla ?
I hope so .
The world will need laughter and courage more than ever in the year that will come next .
I do n't want to preach -- this be n't any time for it .
But I just want to say something that may help you over the bad when you hear that I 've go ` west . '
I 've a premonition about you , Rilla , as well a about myself .
I think Ken will go back to you -- and that there be long year of happiness for you by-and-by .
And you will tell your child of the Idea we fight and die for -- teach them it must be live for as well a die for , else the price pay for it will have be give for nought .
This will be part of your work , Rilla .
And if you -- all you girls back in the homeland -- do it , then we who do n't come back will know that you have not ` broken faith ' with u .
`` I mean to write to Una tonight , too , but I wo n't have time now .
Read this letter to her and tell her it 's really mean for you both -- you two dear , fine loyal girl .
Tomorrow , when we go over the top -- I 'll think of you both -- of your laughter , Rilla-my-Rilla , and the steadfastness in Una 's blue eye -- somehow I see those eye very plainly tonight , too .
Yes , you 'll both keep faith -- I 'm sure of that -- you and Una .
And so -- goodnight .
We go over the top at dawn . ''
Rilla read her letter over many time .
There be a new light on her pale young face when she finally stand up , amid the aster Walter have love , with the sunshine of autumn around her .
For the moment at least , she be lift above pain and loneliness .
`` I will keep faith , Walter , '' she say steadily .
`` I will work -- and teach -- and learn -- and laugh , yes , I will even laugh -- through all my year , because of you and because of what you give when you follow the call . ''
Rilla meant to keep Walter 's letter a a a sacred treasure .
But , see the look on Una Meredith 's face when Una have read it and hold it back to her , she think of something .
Could she do it ?
Oh , no , she could not give up Walter 's letter -- his last letter .
Surely it be not selfishness to keep it .
A copy would be such a soulless thing .
But Una -- Una have so little -- and her eye be the eye of a woman stricken to the heart , who yet must not cry out or ask for sympathy .
`` Una , would you like to have this letter -- to keep ? ''
she ask slowly .
`` Yes -- if you can give it to me , '' Una say dully .
`` Then -- you may have it , '' say Rilla hurriedly .
`` Thank you , '' say Una .
It be all she say , but there be something in her voice which repay Rilla for her bit of sacrifice .
Una take the letter and when Rilla have go she press it against her lonely lip .
Una know that love would never come into her life now -- it be bury for ever under the blood-stained soil `` Somewhere in France . ''
No one but herself -- and perhaps Rilla -- know it -- would ever know it .
She have no right in the eye of her world to grieve .
She must hide and bear her long pain a best she could -- alone .
But she , too , would keep faith .
CHAPTER XXIV MARY IS JUST IN TIME The autumn of 1916 be a bitter season for Ingleside .
Mrs. Blythe 's return to health be slow , and sorrow and loneliness be in all heart .
Every one try to hide it from the others and `` carry on '' cheerfully .
Rilla laugh a good deal .
Nobody at Ingleside be deceive by her laughter ; it come from her lip only , never from her heart .
But outsider say some people get over trouble very easily , and Irene Howard remark that she be surprise to find how shallow Rilla Blythe really be .
`` Why , after all her pose of be so devote to Walter , she do n't seem to mind his death at all .
Nobody have ever see her shed a tear or hear her mention his name .
She have evidently quite forgotten him .
Poor fellow -- you 'd really think his family would feel it more .
I speak of him to Rilla at the last Junior Red meeting -- of how fine and brave and splendid he be -- and I say life could never be just the same to me again , now that Walter have go -- we be such friend , you know -- why I be the very first person he tell about have enlist -- and Rilla answer , a coolly and indifferently a if she be speak of an entire stranger , ` He be just one of many fine and splendid boy who have give everything for their country . '
Well , I wish I could take thing a calmly -- but I 'm not make like that .
I 'm so sensitive -- thing hurt me terribly -- I really never get over them .
I ask Rilla right out why she do n't put on mourn for Walter .
She say her mother do n't wish it .
But every one be talk about it . ''
`` Rilla do n't wear colour -- nothing but white , '' protest Betty Mead .
`` White become her good than anything else , '' say Irene significantly .
`` And we all know black do n't suit her complexion at all .
But of course I 'm not say that be the reason she do n't wear it .
Only , it 's funny .
If my brother have die I 'd have go into deep mourning .
I would n't have have the heart for anything else .
I confess I 'm disappointed in Rilla Blythe . ''
`` I be not , then , '' cry Betty Meade , loyally , `` I think Rilla be just a wonderful girl .
A few year ago I admit I do think she be rather too vain and gigglesome ; but now she be nothing of the sort .
I do n't think there be a girl in the Glen who be so unselfish and plucky a Rilla , or who have do her bit a thoroughly and patiently .
Our Junior Red Cross would have go on the rock a dozen time if it have n't be for her tact and perseverance and enthusiasm -- you know that perfectly well , Irene . ''
`` Why , I be not run Rilla down , '' say Irene , open her eye widely .
`` It be only her lack of feeling I be criticize .
I suppose she ca n't help it .
Of course , she 's a born manager -- everyone know that .
She 's very fond of managing , too -- and people like that be very necessary I admit .
So do n't look at me a if I 'd say something perfectly dreadful , Betty , please .
I 'm quite willing to agree that Rilla Blythe be the embodiment of all the virtue , if that will please you .
And no doubt it be a virtue to be quite unmoved by thing that would crush most people . ''
Some of Irene 's remark be report to Rilla ; but they do not hurt her a they would once have do .
They do n't matter , that be all .
Life be too big to leave room for pettiness .
She have a pact to keep and a work to do ; and through the long hard day and week of that disastrous autumn she be faithful to her task .
The war news be consistently bad , for Germany march from victory to victory over poor Rumania .
`` Foreigners -- foreigner , '' Susan mutter dubiously .
`` Russians or Rumanians or whatever they may be , they be foreigner and you can not tie to them .
But after Verdun I shall not give up hope .
And can you tell me , Mrs. Dr. dear , if the Dobruja be a river or a mountain range , or a condition of the atmosphere ? ''
The Presidential election in the United States come off in November , and Susan be red-hot over that -- and quite apologetic for her excitement .
`` I never think I would live to see the day when I would be interested in a Yankee election , Mrs. Dr. dear .
It only go to show we can never know what we will come to in this world , and therefore we should not be proud . ''
Susan stay up late on the evening of the eleventh , ostensibly to finish a pair of sock .
But she ` phone down to Carter Flagg 's store at interval , and when the first report come through that Hughes have be elect she stalk solemnly upstairs to Mrs. Blythe 's room and announce it in a thrill whisper from the foot of the bed .
`` I think if you be not asleep you would be interested in know it .
I believe it be for the best .
Perhaps he will just fall to write note , too , Mrs. Dr. dear , but I hope for good thing .
I never be very partial to whisker , but one can not have everything . ''
When news come in the morning that after all Wilson be re-elected , Susan tack to catch another breeze of optimism .
`` Well , good a fool you know than a fool you do not know , a the old proverb have it , '' she remark cheerfully .
`` Not that I hold Woodrow to be a fool by any mean , though by time you would not think he have the sense he be bear with .
But he be a good letter writer at least , and we do not know if the Hughes man be even that .
All thing be consider I commend the Yankees .
They have show good sense and I do not mind admit it .
Cousin Sophia want them to elect Roosevelt , and be much disgruntle because they would not give him a chance .
I have a hankering for him myself , but we must believe that Providence over-rules these matter and be satisfy -- though what the Almighty mean in this affair of Rumania I can not fathom -- say it with all reverence . ''
Susan fathom it -- or think she do -- when the Asquith ministry go down and Lloyd George become Premier .
`` Mrs. Dr. dear , Lloyd George be at the helm at last .
I have be pray for this for many a day .
Now we shall soon see a blessed change .
It take the Rumanian disaster to bring it about , no le , and that be the meaning of it , though I could not see it before .
There will be no more shilly-shallying .
I consider that the war be as good a won , and that I shall tie to , whether Bucharest fall or not . ''
Bucharest do fall -- and Germany propose peace negotiation .
Whereat Susan scornfully turn a deaf ear and absolutely refuse to listen to such proposal .
When President Wilson send his famous December peace note Susan wax violently sarcastic .
`` Woodrow Wilson be go to make peace , I understand .
First Henry Ford have a try at it and now come Wilson .
But peace be not make with ink , Woodrow , and that you may tie to , '' say Susan , apostrophize the unlucky President out of the kitchen window nearest the United States .
`` Lloyd George 's speech will tell the Kaiser what be what , and you may keep your peace screed at home and save postage . ''
`` What a pity President Wilson ca n't hear you , Susan , '' say Rilla slyly .
`` Indeed , Rilla dear , it be a pity that he have no one near him to give him good advice , a it be clear he have not , in all those Democrats and Republicans , '' retort Susan .
`` I do not know the difference between them , for the politics of the Yankees be a puzzle I can not solve , study it a I may .
But as far a see through a grindstone go , I be afraid -- '' Susan shake her head dubiously , `` that they be all tar with the same brush . ''
`` I be thankful Christmas be over , '' Rilla write in her diary during the last week of a stormy December .
`` We have dread it so -- the first Christmas since Courcelette .
But we have all the Merediths down for dinner and nobody try to be gay or cheerful .
We be all just quiet and friendly , and that help .
Then , too , I be so thankful that Jims have get well -- so thankful that I almost felt glad -- almost but not quite .
I wonder if I shall ever feel really glad over anything again .
It seem a if gladness be kill in me -- shoot down by the same bullet that pierce Walter 's heart .
Perhaps some day a new kind of gladness will be bear in my soul -- but the old kind will never live again .
`` Winter set in awfully early this year .
Ten day before Christmas we have a big snowstorm -- at least we think it big at the time .
As it happen , it be only a prelude to the real performance .
It be fine the next day , and Ingleside and Rainbow Valley be wonderful , with the tree all cover with snow , and big drift everywhere , carve into the most fantastic shape by the chisel of the northeast wind .
Father and mother go up to Avonlea .
Father think the change would do mother good , and they want to see poor Aunt Diana , whose son Jock have be seriously wound a short time before .
They leave Susan and me to keep house , and father expect to be back the next day .
But he never get back for a week .
That night it begin to storm again , and it storm unbrokenly for four day .
It be the bad and long storm that Prince Edward Island have know for year .
Everything be disorganize -- the road be completely choke up , the train blockade , and the telephone wire put entirely out of commission .
`` And then Jims take ill .
`` He have a little cold when father and mother go away , and he keep get bad for a couple of day , but it do n't occur to me that there be danger of anything serious .
I never even take his temperature , and I ca n't forgive myself , because it be sheer carelessness .
The truth be I have slump just then .
Mother be away , so I let myself go .
All at once I be tire of keep up and pretend to be brave and cheerful , and I just give up for a few day and spend most of the time lie on my face on my bed , cry .
I neglect Jims -- that be the hateful truth -- I be cowardly and false to what I promise Walter -- and if Jims have die I could never have forgive myself .
`` Then , the third night after father and mother go away , Jims suddenly get bad -- oh , so much bad -- all at once .
Susan and I be all alone .
Gertrude have be at Lowbridge when the storm begin and have never get back .
At first we be not much alarm .
Jims have have several bout of croup and Susan and Morgan and I have always bring him through without much trouble .
But it be n't very long before we be dreadfully alarm . '' '
I never saw croup like this before , ' say Susan .
`` As for me , I know , when it be too late , what kind of croup it be .
I know it be not the ordinary croup -- ` false croup ' a doctor call it -- but the ` true croup ' -- and I know that it be a deadly and dangerous thing .
And father be away and there be no doctor near than Lowbridge -- and we could not ` phone and neither horse nor man could get through the drift that night .
`` Gallant little Jims put up a good fight for his life , -- Susan and I try every remedy we could think of or find in father 's book , but he continue to grow bad .
It be heart-rending to see and hear him .
He gasp so horribly for breath -- the poor little soul -- and his face turn a dreadful bluish colour and have such an agonized expression , and he keep struggle with his little hand , a if he be appeal to u to help him somehow .
I find myself think that the boy who have be gas at the front must have look like that , and the thought haunt me amid all my dread and misery over Jims .
And all the time the fatal membrane in his wee throat grow and thicken and he could n't get it up .
`` Oh , I be just wild !
I never realize how dear Jims be to me until that moment .
And I felt so utterly helpless . ''
`` And then Susan give up .
` We can not save him !
Oh , if your father be here -- look at him , the poor little fellow !
I know not what to do . '
`` I look at Jims and I think he be die .
Susan be hold him up in his crib to give him a good chance for breath , but it do n't seem a if he could breathe at all .
My little war-baby , with his dear way and sweet roguish face , be choke to death before my very eyes , and I could n't help him .
I throw down the hot poultice I have ready in despair .
Of what use be it ?
Jims be die , and it be my fault -- I have n't be careful enough !
`` Just then -- at eleven o'clock at night -- the door bell rang .
Such a ring -- it peal all over the house above the roar of the storm .
Susan could n't go -- she dare not lay Jims down -- so I rush downstairs .
In the hall I pause just a minute -- I be suddenly overcome by an absurd dread .
I think of a weird story Gertrude have tell me once .
An aunt of hers be alone in a house one night with her sick husband .
She hear a knock at the door .
And when she go and open it there be nothing there -- nothing that could be see , at least .
But when she open the door a deadly cold wind blew in and seem to sweep past her right up the stair , although it be a calm , warm summer night outside .
Immediately she hear a cry .
She run upstairs -- and her husband be dead .
And she always believe , so Gertrude say , that when she open that door she let Death in .
`` It be so ridiculous of me to feel so frightened .
But I be distract and wear out , and I simply felt for a moment that I dare not open the door -- that death be wait outside .
Then I remember that I have no time to waste -- must not be so foolish -- I spring forward and open the door .
`` Certainly a cold wind do blow in and fill the hall with a whirl of snow .
But there on the threshold stand a form of flesh and blood -- Mary Vance , coat from head to foot with snow -- and she bring Life , not Death , with her , though I do n't know that then .
I just star at her . '' '
I have n't be turn out , ' grin Mary , a she step in and shut the door . '
I come up to Carter Flagg 's two day ago and I 've be stormed-stayed there ever since .
But old Abbie Flagg get on my nerve at last , and tonight I just make up my mind to come up here .
I think I could wade this far , but I can tell you it be as much a a bargain .
Once I think I be stick for keep .
Ai n't it an awful night ? '
`` I come to myself and know I must hurry upstairs .
I explain as quickly a I could to Mary , and leave her try to brush the snow off .
Upstairs I find that Jims be over that paroxysm , but almost as soon a I get back to the room he be in the grip of another .
I could n't do anything but moan and cry -- oh , how ashamed I be when I think of it ; and yet what could I do -- we have try everything we know -- and then all at once I hear Mary Vance say loudly behind me , ` Why , that child be die ! '
`` I whirl around .
Did n't I know he be die -- my little Jims !
I could have throw Mary Vance out of the door or the window -- anywhere -- at that moment .
There she stand , cool and compose , look down at my baby , with those , weird white eye of hers , a she might look at a choke kitten .
I have always dislike Mary Vance -- and just then I hat her . ''
` We have try everything , ' say poor Susan dully .
` It be not ordinary croup . ' ''
` No , it 's the dipthery croup , ' say Mary briskly , snatch up an apron .
` And there 's mighty little time to lose -- but I know what to do .
When I live over-harbour with Mrs. Wiley , year ago , Will Crawford 's kid die of dipthery croup , in spite of two doctor .
And when old Aunt Christina MacAllister heard of it -- she be the one bring me round when I nearly die of pneumonia you know -- she be a wonder -- no doctor be a patch on her -- they do n't hatch her breed of cat nowadays , let me tell you -- she say she could have save him with her grandmother 's remedy if she 'd be there .
She tell Mrs. Wiley what it be and I 've never forget it .
I 've the great memory ever -- a thing just lie in the back of my head till the time come to use it .
Got any sulphur in the house , Susan ? '
`` Yes , we have sulphur .
Susan go down with Mary to get it , and I hold Jims .
I have n't any hope -- not the least .
Mary Vance might brag a she like -- she be always brag -- but I do n't believe any grandmother 's remedy could save Jims now .
Presently Mary come back .
She have tie a piece of thick flannel over her mouth and nose , and she carry Susan 's old tin chip pan , half full of burn coal . ''
` You watch me , ' she say boastfully .
` I 've never do this , but it 's kill or cure that child be die anyway . '
`` She sprinkle a spoonful of sulphur over the coal ; and then she pick up Jims , turn him over , and hold him face downward , right over those choke , blind fume .
I do n't know why I do n't spring forward and snatch him away .
Susan say it be because it be fore-ordained that I should n't , and I think she be right , because it do really seem that I be powerless to move .
Susan herself seem transfixed , watch Mary from the doorway .
Jims writhe in those big , firm , capable hand of Mary -- oh yes , she be capable all right -- and choke and wheeze -- and choke and wheeze -- and I felt that he be be torture to death -- and then all at once , after what seem to me an hour , though it really be n't long , he cough up the membrane that be kill him .
Mary turn him over and lay him back on his bed .
He be white a marble and the tear be pour out of his brown eye -- but that awful livid look be go from his face and he could breathe quite easily . ''
` Was n't that some trick ? '
say Mary gaily . '
I have n't any idea how it would work , but I just take a chance .
I 'll smoke his throat out again once or twice before morning , just to kill all the germ , but you 'll see he 'll be all right now . '
`` Jims go right to sleep -- real sleep , not coma , a I fear at first .
Mary ` smoke him , ' a she call it , twice through the night , and at daylight his throat be perfectly clear and his temperature be almost normal .
When I make sure of that I turn and look at Mary Vance .
She be sit on the lounge lay down the law to Susan on some subject about which Susan must have know forty time as much a she do .
But I do n't mind how much law she lay down or how much she brag .
She have a right to brag -- she have dare to do what I would never have dare , and have save Jims from a horrible death .
It do n't matter any more that she have once chase me through the Glen with a codfish ; it do n't matter that she have smear goose-grease all over my dream of romance the night of the lighthouse dance ; it do n't matter that she think she know more than anybody else and always rub it in -- I would never dislike Mary Vance again .
I go over to her and kiss her . ''
` What 's up now ? '
she say . ''
` Nothing -- only I 'm so grateful to you , Mary . ' ''
` Well , I think you ought to be , that 's a fact .
You two would have let that baby die on your hand if I have n't happen along , ' say Mary , just beam with complacency .
She get Susan and me a tip-top breakfast and make u eat it , and ` boss the life out of u , ' a Susan say , for two day , until the road be open so that she could get home .
Jims be almost well by that time , and father turn up .
He hear our tale without say much .
Father be rather scornful generally about what he call ` old wife ' remedy . '
He laugh a little and say , ` After this , Mary Vance will expect me to call her in for consultation in all my serious case . '
`` So Christmas be not so hard a I expect it to be ; and now the New Year be come -- and we be still hop for the ` Big Push ' that will end the war -- and Little Dog Monday be get stiff and rheumatic from his cold vigil , but still he ` carry on , ' and Shirley continue to read the exploit of the ace .
Oh , nineteen-seventeen , what will you bring ? ''
CHAPTER XXV SHIRLEY GOES `` No , Woodrow , there will be no peace without victory , '' say Susan , stick her knitting needle viciously through President Wilson 's name in the newspaper column .
`` We Canadians mean to have peace and victory , too .
You , if it please you , Woodrow , can have the peace without the victory '' -- and Susan stalk off to bed with the comfortable consciousness of have get the good of the argument with the President .
But a few day later she rush to Mrs. Blythe in red-hot excitement .
`` Mrs. Dr. dear , what do you think ?
A ` phone message have just come through from Charlottetown that Woodrow Wilson have send that German ambassador man to the right about at last .
They tell me that mean war .
So I begin to think that Woodrow 's heart be in the right place after all , wherever his head may be , and I be go to commandeer a little sugar and celebrate the occasion with some fudge , despite the howl of the Food Board .
I think that submarine business would bring thing to a crisis .
I tell Cousin Sophia so when she say it be the beginning of the end for the Allies . ''
`` Do n't let the doctor hear of the fudge , Susan , '' say Anne , with a smile .
`` You know he have lay down very strict rule for u along the line of economy the government have ask for . ''
`` Yes , Mrs. Dr. dear , and a man should be master in his own household , and his woman folk should bow to his decree .
I flatter myself that I be become quite efficient in economize '' -- Susan have take to use certain German term with kill effect -- `` but one can exercise a little gumption on the quiet now and then .
Shirley be wish for some of my fudge the other day -- the Susan brand , a he call it -- and I say ` The first victory there be to celebrate I shall make you some . '
I consider this news quite equal to a victory , and what the doctor do not know will never grieve him .
I take the whole responsibility , Mrs. Dr. dear , so do not you vex your conscience . ''
Susan spoil Shirley shamelessly that winter .
He come home from Queen 's every week-end , and Susan have all his favourite dish for him , in so far a she could evade or wheedle the doctor , and wait on him hand and foot .
Though she talk war constantly to everyone else she never mention it to him or before him , but she watch him like a cat watch a mouse ; and when the German retreat from the Bapaume salient begin and continue , Susan 's exultation be link up with something deep than anything she express .
Surely the end be in sight -- would come now before -- anyone else -- could go .
`` Things be come our way at last .
We have get the Germans on the run , '' she boast .
`` The United States have declare war at last , a I always believe they would , in spite of Woodrow 's gift for letter writing , and you will see they will go into it with a vim since I understand that be their habit , when they do start .
And we have get the Germans on the run , too . ''
`` The States mean well , '' moan Cousin Sophia , `` but all the vim in the world can not put them on the fighting line this spring , and the Allies will be finish before that .
The Germans be just lure them on .
That man Simonds say their retreat have put the Allies in a hole . ''
`` That man Simonds have say more than he will ever live to make good , '' retort Susan .
`` I do not worry myself about his opinion as long a Lloyd George be Premier of England .
He will not be bamboozle and that you may tie to .
Things look good to me .

CHAPTER XXVI SUSAN HAS A PROPOSAL OF MARRIAGE An aeroplane be fly over Glen St. Mary , like a great bird poise against the western sky -- a sky so clear and of such a pale , silvery yellow , that it give an impression of a vast , wind-freshened space of freedom .

Susan be always intensely excite .

But Shirley have go overseas now , so Susan be not so keenly interested in this particular aeroplane and it pilot .
Nevertheless , she look at it with awe .
